</section><h2 class="section-heading" onclick="javascript:mfTempOpenSection(7)"><div class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element indicator mw-ui-icon-small mw-ui-icon-flush-left"></div><span class="mw-headline" id="Environmental_record">Environmental record</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Johnson_%26_Johnson&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Environmental record" data-section="23" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h2><section class="mf-section-7 collapsible-block" id="mf-section-7">
<p>Johnson &amp; Johnson has set several positive goals to keep the company environmentally friendly and was ranked third among the United States's largest companies in <i><a href="/wiki/Newsweek" title="Newsweek">Newsweek</a>'s</i> "Green Rankings".<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup> Some examples are the reduction in water use, waste, and energy use and an increased level of transparency.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup> Johnson &amp; Johnson agreed to change its packaging of plastic bottles used in the manufacturing process, switching their packaging of liquids to non-<a href="/wiki/Polyvinyl_chloride" title="Polyvinyl chloride">polyvinyl chloride</a> containers.<sup id="cite_ref-ens_97-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ens-97">[97]</a></sup> The corporation is working with the Climate Northwest Initiative and the EPA National Environmental Performance Track program.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup> As a member of the national Green Power Partnership, Johnson &amp; Johnson operates the largest solar power generator in Pennsylvania at its site in <a href="/wiki/Spring_House,_Pennsylvania" title="Spring House, Pennsylvania">Spring House, Pennsylvania</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup></p>
</section><h2 class="section-heading" onclick="javascript:mfTempOpenSection(8)"><div class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element indicator mw-ui-icon-small mw-ui-icon-flush-left"></div><span class="mw-headline" id="Recalls_and_litigation">Recalls and litigation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Johnson_%26_Johnson&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Recalls and litigation" data-section="24" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h2><section class="mf-section-8 collapsible-block" id="mf-section-8">
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="1982_Chicago_Tylenol_murders">1982 Chicago Tylenol murders</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Johnson_%26_Johnson&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: 1982 Chicago Tylenol murders" data-section="25" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Chicago_Tylenol_murders" title="Chicago Tylenol murders">Chicago Tylenol murders</a></div>
<p>On September 29, 1982, a "Tylenol scare" began when the first of seven individuals died in <a href="/wiki/Chicago_metropolitan_area" title="Chicago metropolitan area">Chicago metropolitan area</a>, after ingesting Extra Strength Tylenol that had been deliberately laced with <a href="/wiki/Cyanide" title="Cyanide">cyanide</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-nytrecall_100-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nytrecall-100">[100]</a></sup> Within a week, the company pulled 31 million bottles of capsules back from retailers, making it one of the first major recalls in American history.<sup id="cite_ref-nytrecall_100-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nytrecall-100">[100]</a></sup> The incident led to reforms in the packaging of <a href="/wiki/Over-the-counter_substance" class="mw-redirect" title="Over-the-counter substance">over-the-counter substances</a> and to federal anti-tampering laws. The case remains unsolved and no suspects have been charged. Johnson &amp; Johnson's quick response, including a nationwide recall, was widely praised by <a href="/wiki/Public_relations" title="Public relations">public relations</a> experts and the media and was the gold standard for corporate crisis management.<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span id="2010_children.27s_product_recall"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="2010_children's_product_recall">2010 children's product recall</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Johnson_%26_Johnson&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: 2010 children's product recall" data-section="26" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/2010_Johnson_%26_Johnson_children%27s_product_recall" title="2010 Johnson &amp; Johnson children's product recall">2010 Johnson &amp; Johnson children's product recall</a></div>
<p>On April 30, 2010, <a href="/wiki/McNeil_Consumer_Healthcare" title="McNeil Consumer Healthcare">McNeil Consumer Healthcare</a>, a subsidiary of Johnson and Johnson, voluntarily <a href="/wiki/Product_recall" title="Product recall">recalled</a> 43 <a href="/wiki/Over-the-counter_drug" title="Over-the-counter drug">over-the-counter</a> children's medicines, including <a href="/wiki/Tylenol_(brand_redirect)" class="mw-redirect" title="Tylenol (brand redirect)">Tylenol</a>, Tylenol Plus, <a href="/wiki/Motrin" class="mw-redirect" title="Motrin">Motrin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Zyrtec" class="mw-redirect" title="Zyrtec">Zyrtec</a> and <a href="/wiki/Benadryl" title="Benadryl">Benadryl</a>. The recall was conducted after a routine inspection at a manufacturing facility in <a href="/wiki/Fort_Washington,_Pennsylvania" title="Fort Washington, Pennsylvania">Fort Washington, Pennsylvania</a>, United States revealed that some "products may not fully meet the required manufacturing specifications".<sup id="cite_ref-US_firm_recalls_children's_drugs_104-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-US_firm_recalls_children's_drugs-104">[104]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Warning_As_Baby_Medicines_Are_Recalled_105-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Warning_As_Baby_Medicines_Are_Recalled-105">[105]</a></sup> Affected products may contain a "higher concentration of active ingredients" or exhibit other manufacturing defects.<sup id="cite_ref-Warning_As_Baby_Medicines_Are_Recalled_105-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Warning_As_Baby_Medicines_Are_Recalled-105">[105]</a></sup> Products shipped to Canada, Dominican Republic, <a href="/wiki/Guam" title="Guam">Guam</a>, Guatemala, Jamaica, <a href="/wiki/Puerto_Rico" title="Puerto Rico">Puerto Rico</a>, Panama, Trinidad and Tobago, the United Arab Emirates, Kuwait and Fiji were included in the recall.<sup id="cite_ref-US_firm_recalls_children's_drugs_104-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-US_firm_recalls_children's_drugs-104">[104]</a></sup> In a statement, Johnson &amp; Johnson said "a comprehensive quality assessment across its manufacturing operations" was underway.<sup id="cite_ref-US_firm_recalls_children's_drugs_104-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-US_firm_recalls_children's_drugs-104">[104]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Warning_As_Baby_Medicines_Are_Recalled_105-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Warning_As_Baby_Medicines_Are_Recalled-105">[105]</a></sup> A dedicated website was established by the company <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20100504052605/http://www.mcneilproductrecall.com/page.jhtml?id=%2Finclude%2Fnew_recall.inc">listing</a> affected products and other consumer information.<sup id="cite_ref-Warning_As_Baby_Medicines_Are_Recalled_105-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Warning_As_Baby_Medicines_Are_Recalled-105">[105]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="2010_hip-replacement_recall">2010 hip-replacement recall</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Johnson_%26_Johnson&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: 2010 hip-replacement recall" data-section="27" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/2010_DePuy_Hip_Recall" title="2010 DePuy Hip Recall">2010 DePuy Hip Recall</a></div>
<p>On August 24, 2010, DePuy, a subsidiary of American giant Johnson &amp; Johnson, recalled its ASR (articular surface replacement) hip prostheses from the market. DePuy said the recall was due to unpublished National Joint Registry data showing a 12% revision rate for resurfacing at five years and an ASR XL revision rate of 13%. All hip prostheses fail in some patients, but it is expected that the rate will be about 1% a year.<sup id="cite_ref-Out_of_joint_106-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Out_of_joint-106">[106]</a></sup>
Pathologically, the failing prosthesis had several effects. Metal debris from wear of the implant led to a reaction that destroyed the soft tissues surrounding the joint, leaving some patients with long term disability. Ions of cobalt and chromium—the metals from which the implant was made—were also released into the blood and cerebral spinal fluid in some patients.<sup id="cite_ref-fda1_107-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fda1-107">[107]</a></sup></p><p>In March 2013, a jury in Los Angeles ordered Johnson &amp; Johnson to pay more than $8.3 million in damages to a Montana man in the first of more than 10,000 lawsuits pending against the company in connection with the now-recalled DePuy hip.<sup id="cite_ref-Meier_108-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Meier-108">[108]</a></sup></p><p>Some lawyers and industry analysts have estimated that the suits ultimately will cost Johnson &amp; Johnson billions of dollars to resolve.<sup id="cite_ref-Meier_108-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Meier-108">[108]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="2010_Tylenol_recall">2010 Tylenol recall</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Johnson_%26_Johnson&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: 2010 Tylenol recall" data-section="28" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>In 2010 and 2011, Johnson &amp; Johnson voluntarily recalled some <a href="/wiki/Over-the-counter" class="mw-redirect" title="Over-the-counter">over-the-counter</a> products, including Tylenol, due to an odor caused by tribromoanisole.<sup id="cite_ref-WebMd_109-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WebMd-109">[109]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup> In this case, <a href="/wiki/2,4,6-tribromophenol" class="mw-redirect" title="2,4,6-tribromophenol">2,4,6-tribromophenol</a> was used to treat wooden pallets on which product packaging materials were transported and stored.<sup id="cite_ref-WebMd_109-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WebMd-109">[109]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Shareholders_lawsuit">Shareholders lawsuit</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Johnson_%26_Johnson&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Shareholders lawsuit" data-section="29" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>In 2010 a group of <a href="/wiki/Shareholders" class="mw-redirect" title="Shareholders">shareholders</a> sued the board for allegedly failing to take action to prevent serious failings and illegalities since the 1990s, including manufacturing problems, bribing officials, covering up adverse effects and misleading marketing for unapproved uses. The judge initially dismissed the case in September 2011, but allowed the plaintiffs opportunity to refile at a later time.<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup> In 2012 Johnson and Johnson proposed a settlement with the shareholders, whereby the company would institute new oversight, quality and compliance procedures binding for five years.<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Illegal_marketing_of_Risperdal">Illegal marketing of Risperdal</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Johnson_%26_Johnson&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Illegal marketing of Risperdal" data-section="30" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>Juries in several US states have found J&amp;J guilty of concealing the <a href="/wiki/Adverse_effects" class="mw-redirect" title="Adverse effects">adverse effects</a> of <a href="/wiki/Janssen_Pharmaceuticals" class="mw-redirect" title="Janssen Pharmaceuticals">Janssen Pharmaceuticals</a>' <a href="/wiki/Antipsychotic" title="Antipsychotic">antipsychotic</a> medication <a href="/wiki/Risperdal" class="mw-redirect" title="Risperdal">Risperdal</a>, produced by its unit, in order to promote it to doctors and patients as better than cheaper generics, and of falsely marketing it for treating patients with <a href="/wiki/Dementia" title="Dementia">dementia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-CNNMoney_113-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CNNMoney-113">[113]</a></sup> States that have awarded damages include <a href="/wiki/Texas" title="Texas">Texas</a> ($158 million), <a href="/wiki/South_Carolina" title="South Carolina">South Carolina</a> ($327 million), <a href="/wiki/Louisiana" title="Louisiana">Louisiana</a> ($258 million), and most notably <a href="/wiki/Arkansas" title="Arkansas">Arkansas</a> ($1.2 billion).<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup></p><p>In 2010, the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Justice" title="United States Department of Justice">United States Department of Justice</a> joined a <a href="/wiki/Whistleblowers" class="mw-redirect" title="Whistleblowers">whistleblowers</a> suit accusing the company of illegally marketing Risperdal through <a href="/wiki/Omnicare" title="Omnicare">Omnicare</a>, the largest company supplying pharmaceuticals to nursing homes.<sup id="cite_ref-Hilzenrath_115-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hilzenrath-115">[115]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup> The allegations include that J&amp;J were warned by the U.S. <a href="/wiki/Food_and_Drug_Administration" title="Food and Drug Administration">Food and Drug Administration</a> (FDA) not to promote Risperdal as effective and safe for elderly patients, but they did so, and that they paid Omnicare to promote the drug to care home physicians.<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup> The settlement was finalized on November 4, 2013, with J&amp;J agreeing to pay a penalty of around $2.2 billion, "including criminal fines and forfeiture totaling $485 million and civil settlements with the federal government and states totaling $1.72 billion".<sup id="cite_ref-DOH2013_118-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DOH2013-118">[118]</a></sup></p><p>Johnson &amp; Johnson has also been subject to congressional investigations related to payments given to <a href="/wiki/Psychiatrists" class="mw-redirect" title="Psychiatrists">psychiatrists</a> to promote its products and <a href="/wiki/Ghost_writer" class="mw-redirect" title="Ghost writer">ghost write</a> articles, notably <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Biederman" title="Joseph Biederman">Joseph Biederman</a> and his pediatric <a href="/wiki/Bipolar_disorder" title="Bipolar disorder">bipolar disorder</a> research unit.<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Foreign_bribery">Foreign bribery</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Johnson_%26_Johnson&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Foreign bribery" data-section="31" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>In 2011, J&amp;J settled litigation brought by the <a href="/wiki/US_Securities_and_Exchange_Commission" class="mw-redirect" title="US Securities and Exchange Commission">US Securities and Exchange Commission</a> under the <a href="/wiki/Foreign_Corrupt_Practices_Act" title="Foreign Corrupt Practices Act">Foreign Corrupt Practices Act</a> and paid around $70M in disgorgement and fines.<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup> J&amp;J's employees had given kickbacks and bribes to doctors in Greece, Poland, and Romania to obtain business selling drugs and medical devices and had bribed officials in <a href="/wiki/Iraq" title="Iraq">Iraq</a> to win contracts under the Oil for Food program.<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup> J&amp;J fully cooperated with the investigation once the problems came to light.<sup id="cite_ref-FCPA2011_122-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FCPA2011-122">[122]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Consumer_fraud_settlements">Consumer fraud settlements</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Johnson_%26_Johnson&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Consumer fraud settlements" data-section="32" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>In May 2017, J&amp;J reached an agreement to pay $33 million to several states to settle consumer fraud allegations in some of the company's over-the-counter drugs.<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Use_of_the_Red_Cross_symbol">Use of the Red Cross symbol</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Johnson_%26_Johnson&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Use of the Red Cross symbol" data-section="33" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Emblems_of_the_International_Red_Cross_and_Red_Crescent_Movement#Use_of_the_emblems" title="Emblems of the International Red Cross and Red Crescent Movement">Emblems of the International Red Cross and Red Crescent Movement § Use of the emblems</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Flag_of_the_Red_Cross.svg" class="image"><noscript><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Flag_of_the_Red_Cross.svg/150px-Flag_of_the_Red_Cross.svg.png" decoding="async" width="150" height="100" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="900" data-file-height="600"></noscript><span class="lazy-image-placeholder" style="width: 150px;height: 100px;" data-src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Flag_of_the_Red_Cross.svg/150px-Flag_of_the_Red_Cross.svg.png" data-alt="" data-width="150" data-height="100" data-srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Flag_of_the_Red_Cross.svg/225px-Flag_of_the_Red_Cross.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Flag_of_the_Red_Cross.svg/300px-Flag_of_the_Red_Cross.svg.png 2x" data-class="thumbimage"> </span></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Flag_of_the_Red_Cross.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Flag of the <a href="/wiki/Red_Cross" class="mw-redirect" title="Red Cross">Red Cross</a></div></div></div>
<p>Johnson &amp; Johnson registered the <a href="/wiki/Red_Cross" class="mw-redirect" title="Red Cross">Red Cross</a> as a U.S. trademark for "medicinal and surgical plasters" in 1905 and has used the design since 1887.<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Geneva_Conventions" title="Geneva Conventions">Geneva Conventions</a>, which reserved the <a href="/wiki/Emblems_of_the_International_Red_Cross_and_Red_Crescent_Movement#Red_Cross" title="Emblems of the International Red Cross and Red Crescent Movement">Red Cross emblem</a> for specific uses, were first approved in 1864 and ratified by the United States in 1882. However, the emblem was not protected by U.S. law for the use of the <a href="/wiki/American_Red_Cross" title="American Red Cross">American Red Cross</a> and the U.S. military until after Johnson &amp; Johnson had obtained its trademark. A clause in this law (now 18 U.S.C. 706) permits this pre-existing use of the Red Cross to continue.
</p><p>A declaration made by the U.S. upon its ratification of the 1949 Geneva Conventions includes a <a href="/wiki/Reservation_(law)" title="Reservation (law)">reservation</a> that pre-1905 U.S. domestic uses of the Red Cross, such as Johnson &amp; Johnson's, would remain lawful as long as the cross is not used on "aircraft, vessels, vehicles, buildings or other structures, or upon the ground," i.e., uses which could be confused with its military uses.<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup> This means that the U.S. did not agree to any interpretation of the 1949 Geneva Conventions that would overrule Johnson &amp; Johnson's trademark. The American Red Cross continues to recognize the validity of Johnson &amp; Johnson's trademark.<sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup></p><p>In August 2007, Johnson &amp; Johnson filed a lawsuit against the <a href="/wiki/American_Red_Cross" title="American Red Cross">American Red Cross</a> (ARC), demanding that the charity halt the use of the red cross symbol on products it sells to the public, though the company takes no issue with the charity's use of the mark for non-profit purposes.<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup> In May 2008, the judge in the case dismissed most of Johnson &amp; Johnson's claims, and a month later the two organizations announced a settlement had been reached in which both parties would continue to use the symbol.<sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Boston_Scientific_lawsuits">Boston Scientific lawsuits</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Johnson_%26_Johnson&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Boston Scientific lawsuits" data-section="34" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>Since 2003, Johnson &amp; Johnson and <a href="/wiki/Boston_Scientific" title="Boston Scientific">Boston Scientific</a> have both claimed that the other had infringed on their patents covering <a href="/wiki/Heart_stent" class="mw-redirect" title="Heart stent">heart stent</a> medical devices. The litigation was settled when Boston Scientific agreed to pay $716 million to Johnson &amp; Johnson in September 2009 and an additional $1.73 billion in February 2010.<sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup> Their dispute was renewed in 2014, now on the grounds of a contract dispute.<sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Patent-infringement_case_against_Abbott">Patent-infringement case against Abbott</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Johnson_%26_Johnson&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Patent-infringement case against Abbott" data-section="35" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>In 2007, Johnson &amp; Johnson sued <a href="/wiki/Abbott_Laboratories" title="Abbott Laboratories">Abbott Laboratories</a> over the development and sale of the arthritis drug <a href="/wiki/Humira" class="mw-redirect" title="Humira">Humira</a>. Johnson &amp; Johnson claimed that Abbott used technology patented by <a href="/wiki/New_York_University" title="New York University">New York University</a> and licensed exclusively to Johnson &amp; Johnson's <a href="/wiki/Centocor" class="mw-redirect" title="Centocor">Centocor</a> division to develop Humira. Johnson &amp; Johnson won the court case, and in 2009 Abbott was ordered to pay Johnson &amp; Johnson $1.17 billion in lost revenues and $504 million in royalties.<sup id="cite_ref-Bloomberg2009_133-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bloomberg2009-133">[133]</a></sup> The judge also added $175.6 million in interest to bring the total to $1.84 billion.<sup id="cite_ref-bloomberg2010_134-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bloomberg2010-134">[134]</a></sup> This was the largest patent-infringement award in U.S. history<sup id="cite_ref-Bloomberg2009_133-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bloomberg2009-133">[133]</a></sup> until the 2013 decision against Teva in favor of <a href="/wiki/Takeda" title="Takeda">Takeda</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pfizer" title="Pfizer">Pfizer</a> for over 2.1 billion dollars.<sup id="cite_ref-WSJ2013_135-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WSJ2013-135">[135]</a></sup> In 2010 Abbott appealed the verdict<sup id="cite_ref-bloomberg2010_134-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bloomberg2010-134">[134]</a></sup> and in 2011 won the appeal.<sup id="cite_ref-BusinessWeek2011_136-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BusinessWeek2011-136">[136]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Vaginal_mesh_implants">Vaginal mesh implants</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Johnson_%26_Johnson&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Vaginal mesh implants" data-section="36" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>Tens of thousands of women worldwide have taken legal action against Johnson &amp; Johnson after suffering serious complications following a vaginal mesh implant procedure.<sup id="cite_ref-cbsnews.com_137-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cbsnews.com-137">[137]</a></sup> In 2016 the U.S. states of <a href="/wiki/California" title="California">California</a> and <a href="/wiki/Washington_(U.S._state)" class="mw-redirect" title="Washington (U.S. state)">Washington</a> filed a lawsuit against the company, accusing it of deception.<sup id="cite_ref-cbsnews.com_137-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cbsnews.com-137">[137]</a></sup> More than 700 women began a <a href="/wiki/Class_action" title="Class action">class action</a> against the company in the <a href="/wiki/Federal_Court_of_Australia" title="Federal Court of Australia">Federal Court of Australia</a> in 2017, telling the court they "suffered irreparable, debilitating pain after the devices began to erode into surrounding tissue and organs, causing infections and complications". The class action alleged that Johnson &amp; Johnson, which "aggressively marketed" the implants "failed to properly warn patients and surgeons of the risk, or test the devices adequately".<sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">[138]</a></sup> Emails between executives show the company was aware of the risks in 2005 but still went ahead and made the product available.<sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139">[139]</a></sup></p><p>In October 2019, the company and its subsidiary, Ethicon, Inc. reached a settlement with 41 states and the District of Columbia, with no admission of liability, in a suit alleging deceptive marketing of transvaginal surgical-mesh devices. The suit also alleges that the company failed to disclose risks associated with the product, which J&amp;J pulled from the market in 2012. The amount settled in the suit was about US$117 million.<sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140">[140]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Baby_powder">Baby powder</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Johnson_%26_Johnson&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Baby powder" data-section="37" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>In February 2016, J&amp;J was ordered to pay $72 million in damages to the family of Jacqueline Fox, a 62-year-old woman who died of ovarian cancer in 2015: the company said it would appeal.<sup id="cite_ref-BBC_141-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC-141">[141]</a></sup></p><p>By March 2017, over 1,000 U.S. women had sued J&amp;J for covering up the possible cancer risk from its Baby Powder product; the company says that 70 percent of its Baby Powder is used by adults.<sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup></p><p>In August 2017, a California jury ordered Johnson &amp; Johnson to pay $417 million to a woman who claimed she developed ovarian cancer after using the company's talc-based products like Johnson's Baby Powder for feminine hygiene. The verdict included $70 million in compensatory damages and $347 million in punitive damages. J&amp;J said they will appeal the verdict.<sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">[143]</a></sup></p><p>In October 2017, the <a href="/wiki/Missouri" title="Missouri">Missouri</a> Eastern District appeals court on Tuesday threw out a $72 million jury verdict. The appeals court ruled 3-0 that Jacqueline Fox's lawsuit lacked jurisdiction in Missouri because of a U.S. Supreme Court decision that imposed limits on where injury lawsuit can be filed which ". . . establishing a lawsuit's jurisdiction requires a stronger connection between the forum state and a plaintiff's claims."  Subsequently, this ruling would kill three other recent St. Louis jury verdicts of more than $200 million combined. Fox, 62, of <a href="/wiki/Birmingham,_Ala." class="mw-redirect" title="Birmingham, Ala.">Birmingham, Ala.</a>, died in 2015, about four months before her trial was held in <a href="/wiki/St._Louis" title="St. Louis">St. Louis</a> Circuit Court. She was among 65 plaintiffs, of whom only two were from Missouri.<sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144">[144]</a></sup></p><p>In July 2018, a St. Louis jury awarded nearly US$4.7 billion in damages to 22 women and their families after they claimed asbestos in Johnson &amp; Johnson talcum powder caused their ovarian cancer.<sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145">[145]</a></sup>  Conversely, in December 2019, a St. Louis jury ruled in favor of Johnson &amp; Johnson in the case of a single plaintiff who had used the company's talc-containing baby powder for thirty years with a similar claim.<sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146">[146]</a></sup>  In 2019, the company's CEO, Alex Gorsky, declined to appear at a United States congressional hearing on the safety of the Johnson's and Johnson's Baby Powder and other talc-based cosmetics. J&amp;J spokesman Ernie Knewitz said that the subcommittee had rejected the company's offers to send a talc testing expert or a J&amp;J executive in charge of consumer products.<sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147">[147]</a></sup></p><p>In August 2018, Johnson &amp; Johnson said that it removed several chemicals from baby powder products and re-engineered them to make consumers more confident that products were safer for children.<sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148">[148]</a></sup></p><p>In December 2018, with 11,700 people suing Johnson &amp; Johnson over cancers allegedly caused by baby powder, the company was forced to release internal documents. The documents showed that the company had known about asbestos contamination since at least as early as 1971 and had spent decades finding ways to conceal the evidence from the public.<sup id="cite_ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149">[149]</a></sup> On December 19, the company lost its request to reverse a jury verdict that ruled in favor of the accusers, which required the company to pay $4.14 billion in punitive damages and $550 million in compensatory damages.<sup id="cite_ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150">[150]</a></sup> Though <a href="/wiki/Asbestos" title="Asbestos">asbestos</a> is a known <a href="/wiki/Carcinogen" title="Carcinogen">carcinogen</a>, the potential link between asbestos-free talc and cancer also alleged in these lawsuits is a subject of scientific controversy, as discussed on the Neurologica blog by <a href="/wiki/Steven_Novella" title="Steven Novella">Steven Novella</a>. A large study performed in 2003 found that ovarian cancer risk increased from a baseline of 0.0121% to 0.0161% in people who reported regularly using talc in the genital area. Two more studies over the next twelve years, which also relied on <a href="/wiki/Self-report" class="mw-redirect" title="Self-report">self-reporting</a>, had similar results. However, none of the three studies showed a relationship between how long someone used talc and how much their cancer risk increased, which is expected in experiments with carcinogens and other toxic substances (see <a href="/wiki/Dose-response_relationship" class="mw-redirect" title="Dose-response relationship">dose-response relationship</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-151" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-151">[151]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Opioid_epidemic">Opioid epidemic</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Johnson_%26_Johnson&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Opioid epidemic" data-section="38" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Opioid_epidemic_in_the_United_States" title="Opioid epidemic in the United States">Opioid epidemic in the United States</a></div>
<p>By 2018, the company had become embroiled in the <a href="/wiki/Opioid_epidemic_in_the_United_States" title="Opioid epidemic in the United States">opioid epidemic in the United States</a> and had become a target of <a href="/wiki/Lawsuit" title="Lawsuit">lawsuits</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-152" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-152">[152]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-153" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-153">[153]</a></sup> Over 500 opioid-related cases have been filed as of May 2018 against Johnson &amp; Johnson and its competitors.<sup id="cite_ref-154" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-154">[154]</a></sup> In Idaho, Johnson &amp; Johnson is part of a lawsuit accusing the company for being partially to blame for opioid-related overdose deaths.<sup id="cite_ref-155" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-155">[155]</a></sup> The first major trial began in Oklahoma in May 2019.<sup id="cite_ref-156" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-156">[156]</a></sup> On August 26, 2019, the Oklahoma judge ordered Johnson &amp; Johnson to pay $572 million for their part in the opioid crisis.<sup id="cite_ref-157" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-157">[157]</a></sup> In October 2019, Johnson &amp; Johnson reached a $20.4 million settlement with two Ohio counties grappling with the opioid epidemic.<sup id="cite_ref-158" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-158">[158]</a></sup></p>
<h4 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Northeastern_Ohio_Settlement">Northeastern Ohio Settlement</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Johnson_%26_Johnson&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Northeastern Ohio Settlement" data-section="39" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h4>
<p>In October 2019, the company agreed to a settlement of US$20.4 million with two Ohio counties – Cuyahoga (Cleveland) and Summit (Akron). The settlement allows the company avoidance of a trial accusing J&amp;J and many other pharmaceutical manufacturers of helping to spark the US opioid epidemic. The trial, scheduled for October 2019, was thought to be an indicator for thousands of opioid-related lawsuits against many drug manufacturers. The arrangement, which contains no admission of liability by the company, provides the counties US$10 million in cash, $5 million for legal expenses and $5.4 million in contributions to opioid-related non-profit organizations in the counties.<sup id="cite_ref-159" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-159">[159]</a></sup></p>
</section><h2 class="section-heading" onclick="javascript:mfTempOpenSection(9)"><div class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element indicator mw-ui-icon-small mw-ui-icon-flush-left"></div><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Johnson_%26_Johnson&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: See also" data-section="40" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h2><section class="mf-section-9 collapsible-block" id="mf-section-9">
